               </script>\n            </div>\n        \n        \n            <h1 class=\"main-title md-title mt3_col-12 mt3_col-lg-9\"><span itemprop=\"headline\">What Makes a Genius?</span>\n            </h1>\n        \n        <div class=\"upper-right-ad mt3_col-12 mt3_col-lg-3\">\n            <div id='upper-right-ad' class=\"ad-holder\" data-ad-component=\"single\" data-ad-config-key=\"sponsorAd_upperRight\"></div>\n        </div>\n        \n            <div class=\"article__deck mt3_col-12 mt3_col-lg-9\">\n                <h2>Some minds are so exceptional they change the world. We don\u2019t know exactly why these people soar above the rest of us, but science offers us clues.</h2>\n            </div>\n        \n    </div>\n</div>\n\n</div>\n\n</div>\n\n       \n\n        \n            <figcaption class=\"media__caption \">\n                <div class=\"media__caption--text\">\n                    \n                    \n                        <span itemprop=\"caption\">The truest measure of genius is whether a person\u2019s work resonates through the ages. At the Galleria dell\u2019Accademia in Florence, Italy, Michelangelo\u2019s \u201cDavid\u201d towers over admiring visitors more than 500 years after the artist carved the 17-foot-tall statue from a single block of marble discarded by other sculptors.</span>\n                    \n                    \n                    \n                </div>\n            </figcaption>\n        \n\n        \n        \n        \n\n    </figure>\n\n\n\t\n\t\n\t    </div>\n        <span class=\"bumper--top bumper--top_addthis\"></span>\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\n</div>\n<div class=\"section byline\">      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    <div id=\"44jhz7zs\" data-platform-component=\"Byline\" data-platform-hide-bylines=\"false\" data-platform-hide-publish-date=\"true\" data-platform-publish-date-format=\"ngm\"></div>\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<blockquote>\n This story appears in the \n <a href=\"http://www.nationalgeographic.com/magazine/2017/05/\" target=\"_blank\">May 2017</a> issue of \n <i>National Geographic</i> magazine.\n <br /> \n</blockquote></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p><b>The M&uuml;tter Museum in Philadelphia</b> houses an array of singular medical specimens. On the lower level the fused livers of 19th-century conjoined twins Chang and Eng float in a glass vessel. Nearby, visitors can gawk at hands swollen with gout, the bladder stones of Chief Justice John Marshall, the cancerous tumor extracted from President Grover Cleveland\u2019s jaw, and a thighbone from a Civil War soldier with the wounding bullet still in place. But there\u2019s one exhibit near the entrance that elicits unmatchable awe. Look closely at the display, and you can see smudge marks left by museumgoers pressing their foreheads against the glass.</p></div>\n<div class=\"promo section relatedContentList\">      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n        <div class=\"bumper--bottom_addthis\"></div>\n        <div class=\"related-content-list\">\n            <h6 class=\"related-content-list__title\">\n                \n                    \n                        <p>Coming to NG Channel</p>\n\n                    \n                    \n                \n            </h6>\n            <div class=\"related-content-list__body\">\n                \n                    \n                \n                <ul>\n                    \n                        <li>\n                            <span itemprop=\"relatedLink\"><a href=\"http://channel.nationalgeographic.com/\" target=\"_blank\" itemprop=\"url\">Learn more about &ldquo;Genius,&rdquo; premiering April 25, 9&#x2f;8 C </a></span>\n                        </li>\n                    \n                </ul>\n                \n            </div>\n            <div class=\"bumper--top_addthis bumper--add-margin\"></div>\n        </div>\n    \n    \n\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>The object that fascinates them is a small wooden box containing 46 microscope slides, each displaying a slice of Albert Einstein\u2019s brain. A magnifying glass positioned over one of the slides reveals a piece of tissue about the size of a stamp, its graceful branches and curves resembling an aerial view of an estuary. These remnants of brain tissue are mesmerizing even though\u2014or perhaps because\u2014they reveal little about the physicist\u2019s vaunted powers of cognition. Other displays in the museum show disease and disfigurement\u2014the results of something gone wrong. Einstein\u2019s brain represents potential, the ability of one exceptional mind, one genius, to catapult ahead of everyone else. \u201cHe saw differently from the rest of us,\u201d says visitor Karen O\u2019Hair as she peers at the tea-colored sample. \u201cAnd he could extend beyond that to what he couldn\u2019t see, which is absolutely amazing.\u201d</p></div>\n<div class=\"imageGroup section\">\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    <span class=\" bumper--bottom bumper--add-margin bumper--bottom_addthis\"></span>\n    <div class=\"cinematic-element fullsize-element clearfix \">\n\n\n\n    <div data-pestle-module=\"ImageGroup\">\n        <script type=\"text/json\" data-pestle-options>\n            {\"id\":\"e8747fab-2ef6-4230-af6a-c82297ee94db/content/imagegroup\",\"pagewidePresentationDisabled\":false,\"hideGutter\":false,\"hideForMobile\":false,\"disableEnlarge\":false,\"size\":\"cinematic\",\"caption\":\"<p>Albert Einstein epitomizes genius, which has led to an abiding interest in his brain. In 1951 the physicist\\u2019s brain waves were recorded; after his death in 1955, a pathologist mounted and dyed slices of it on glass slides. Many of those slides (right) are at the National Museum of Health and Medicine in Silver Spring, Maryland.<\\/p>\",\"image1Path\":\"http://www.nationalgeographic.com/content/dam/magazine/rights-exempt/2017/05/Genius/genius2-albert-einstein-black-white-portrait.jpg\",\"image1Caption\":\"<p>Albert Einstein epitomizes genius, which has led to an abiding interest in his brain. In 1951 the physicist\\u2019s brain waves were recorded; after his death in 1955, a pathologist mounted and dyed slices of it on glass slides. Many of those slides (right) are at the National Museum of Health and Medicine in Silver Spring, Maryland.<\\/p>\",\"image1Credit\":\"Photograph by Philippe Halsman, Magnum Photos\",\"image1AltText\":\"Picture of Albert Einstein in black and white.\",\"image1AspectRatio\":1.4993006993006992,\"image1Guid\":\"af80ab23-e2e7-4ca6-bf4a-d546f3f74bf9\",\"image2Path\":\"http://www.nationalgeographic.com/content/dam/magazine/rights-exempt/2017/05/Genius/genius3-slides-albert-einstein-brain-museum.JPG\",\"image2Caption\":\"<p>Albert Einstein epitomizes genius, which has led to an abiding interest in his brain. In 1951 the physicist\\u2019s brain waves were recorded; after his death in 1955, a pathologist mounted and dyed slices of it on glass slides. Many of those slides (right) are at the National Museum of Health and Medicine in Silver Spring, Maryland.<\\/p>\",\"image2AltText\":\"Picture of glass slides with mounted and dyed slices of Albert Einstein's brain.\",\"image2AspectRatio\":1.501466275659824,\"image2Guid\":\"5bd1243e-7dae-45ae-8a05-38b7bcc07a85\"}\n        </script>\n    </div>\n\n\n\n\n    </div>\n    <span class=\"bumper--top bumper--top_addthis\"></span>\n\n\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Throughout history rare individuals have stood out for their meteoric contributions to a field. Lady Murasaki for her literary inventiveness. Michelangelo for his masterful touch. Marie Curie for her scientific acuity. \u201cThe genius,\u201d wrote German philosopher Arthur Schopenhauer, \u201clights on his age like a comet into the paths of the planets.\u201d Consider Einstein\u2019s impact on physics. With no tools at his disposal other than the force of his own thoughts, he predicted in his general theory of relativity that massive accelerating objects\u2014like black holes orbiting each other\u2014would create ripples in the fabric of space-time. It took one hundred years, enormous computational power, and massively sophisticated technology to definitively prove him right, with the physical detection of such gravitational waves less than two years ago.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Einstein revolutionized our understanding of the very laws of the universe. But our understanding of how a mind like his works remains stubbornly earthbound. What set his brainpower, his thought processes, apart from those of his merely brilliant peers? What makes a genius?</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Philosophers have long been pondering the origins of genius. Early Greek thinkers believed an overabundance of black bile\u2014one of the four bodily humors proposed by Hippocrates\u2014endowed poets, philosophers, and other eminent souls with \u201cexalted powers,\u201d says historian Darrin McMahon, author of <i>Divine Fury: A History of Genius.</i> Phrenologists attempted to find genius in bumps on the head; craniometrists collected skulls\u2014including philosopher Immanuel Kant\u2019s\u2014which they probed, measured, and weighed.</p></div>\n<div class=\"rightRailSlot section\">      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n<div class=\"right-rail-slot bumper--bottom bumper--add-margin\" style=\"margin-top:px\">\n    <div class=\"rightRailContent parsys\">\n</div>\n\n    <span class=\"bumper--top bumper--add-margin\"></span>\n</div></div>\n<div class=\"image parbase section\">\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n        <span class=\"bumper--bottom bumper--add-margin bumper--bottom_addthis\"></span>\n        <div class=\"fullsize-element \">\n            <div class=\"media--medium__container\">\n    \n\n    \n    \n\n    <figure itemscope=\"itemscope\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"image media-image media--medium left   \">\n\n        \n            <div id=\"o1skxhxa\" data-pagewide-presentation-disabled=\"false\">\n                \n                    \n                        <div class=\"placeholder-image-wrap\" style=\"padding-bottom: 75.0%\">\n                            <div class=\"picturefill\" data-pestle-module=\"PictureFill\">\n  <script type=\"text/json\" data-pestle-options>\n  {\"alt\":\"Picture of scientist on bicycle riding along underground       \n\n        \n            <figcaption class=\"media__caption medium-caption\">\n                <div class=\"media__caption--text\">\n                    \n                    \n                        <span itemprop=\"caption\">A century after Einstein predicted the existence of gravitational waves\u2014ripples in the fabric of space-time\u2014 in his general theory of relativity, scientists like Kazuhiro Yamamoto (on bicycle) plan to use the first underground gravitational wave telescope, KAGRA, in Hida, Japan, to explore what he deduced but could not detect.</span>\n                    \n                    \n                    \n                </div>\n            </figcaption>\n        \n\n        \n        \n        \n\n    </figure>\n\n\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t         </div>\n\t    </div>\n        <span class=\"bumper--top bumper--top_addthis\"></span>\n\t\n\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>None of them discovered a single source of genius, and such a thing is unlikely to be found. Genius is too elusive, too subjective, too wedded to the verdict of history to be easily identified. And it requires the ultimate expression of too many traits to be simplified into the highest point on one human scale. Instead we can try to understand it by unraveling the complex and tangled qualities\u2014intelligence, creativity, perseverance, and simple good fortune, to name a few\u2014that entwine to create a person capable of changing the world.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p><b>Intelligence has often </b>been considered the default yardstick of genius\u2014a measurable quality generating tremendous accomplishment. Lewis Terman, the Stanford University psychologist who helped pioneer the IQ test, believed a test that captured intelligence would also reveal genius. In the 1920s he began tracking more than 1,500 Californian schoolkids with IQs generally above 140\u2014a threshold he labeled as \u201cnear genius or genius\u201d\u2014to see how they fared in life and how they compared with other children. Terman and his collaborators followed the participants, nicknamed \u201cTermites,\u201d for their lifetimes and mapped their successes in a series of reports, <i>Genetic Studies of Genius.</i> The group included members of the National Academy of Sciences, politicians, doctors, professors, and musicians. Forty years after the study began, the researchers documented the thousands of academic reports and books they published, as well as the number of patents granted (350) and short stories written (about 400).</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>But monumental intelligence on its own is no guarantee of monumental achievement, as Terman and his collaborators would discover. A number of the study\u2019s participants struggled to thrive, despite their towering IQ scores. Several dozen flunked out of college at first. Others, tested for the study but with IQs that weren\u2019t high enough to make the cut, grew up to become renowned in their fields, most famously Luis Alvarez and William Shockley, both of whom won Nobel Prizes in physics. There\u2019s precedent for such underestimation: Charles Darwin recalled being considered \u201ca very ordinary boy, rather below the common standard in intellect.\u201d As an adult he solved the mystery of how the splendid diversity of life came into being.</p></div>\n<div class=\"image parbase section\">\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n\n    \n    \n        <span class=\" bumper--bottom bumper--add-margin bumper--bottom_addthis\"></span>\n        <div class=\"cinematic-element fullsize-element \">\n    \n\n    <figure itemscope=\"itemscope\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"image media-image media--cinematic left   \">\n\n        \n            <div id=\"qh32zwtb\" data-pagewide-presentation-disabled=\"false\">\n                \n                    \n                        <div class=\"placeholder-image-wrap\" style=\"padding-bottom: 79.98046875%\">\n                            <div class=\"picturefill\" data-pestle-module=\"PictureFill\">\n  <script type=\"text/json\" data-pestle-options>\n  {\"alt\":\"Picture of apple tree that sparked Newton&#039;s law of gravity by his home at Woolsthorpe Manor,  </script>\n</div>\n                        </div>\n                    \n                    \n                \n            \n                <script language=\"javascript\">\n class=\"mt3_icon\">\n                        <use xlink:href=\"#gallery\"></use>\n                      <svg>\n                    </span>\n                </span>\n            \n            </div>\n        \n\n        \n            <figcaption class=\"media__caption \">\n                <div class=\"media__caption--text\">\n                    \n                    \n                        <span itemprop=\"caption\">Unexpected flashes of insight still require some thought. After seeing an apple fall perpendicularly to the ground in 1666, Isaac Newton reasoned that, in a friend\u2019s telling, \u201cthere must be a drawing power in matter.\u201d The tree that sparked his law of gravity remains rooted next to his childhood home at Woolsthorpe Manor, England.</span>\n                    \n                    \n                    \n                </div>\n            </figcaption>\n        \n\n        \n        \n        \n\n    </figure>\n\n\n\t\n\t\n\t    </div>\n        <span class=\"bumper--top bumper--top_addthis\"></span>\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Scientific breakthroughs like Darwin\u2019s theory of evolution by natural selection would be impossible without creativity, a strand of genius that Terman couldn\u2019t measure. But creativity and its processes can be explained, to a certain extent, by creative people themselves. Scott Barry Kaufman, scientific director of the Imagination Institute in Philadelphia, has been bringing together individuals who stand out as trailblazers in their fields\u2014people like psychologist Steven Pinker and comedian Anne Libera of the Second City\u2014to talk about how their ideas and insights are kindled. Kaufman\u2019s goal is not to elucidate genius\u2014he considers the word to be a societal judgment that elevates a chosen few while overlooking others\u2014but to nurture imagination in everyone.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>These discussions have revealed that the aha moment, the flash of clarity that arises at unexpected times\u2014in a dream, in the shower, on a walk\u2014often emerges after a period of contemplation. Information comes in consciously, but the problem is processed unconsciously, the resulting solution leaping out when the mind least expects it. \u201cGreat ideas don\u2019t tend to come when you\u2019re narrowly focusing on them,\u201d says Kaufman.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Studies of the brain offer hints at how these aha moments might happen. The creative process, says Rex Jung, a neuroscientist at the University of New Mexico, relies on the dynamic interplay of neural networks operating in concert and drawing from different parts of the brain at once\u2014both the right and left hemispheres and especially regions in the prefrontal cortex. One of these networks fosters our ability to meet external demands\u2014activities we must act on, like going to work and paying our taxes\u2014and resides largely in outer areas of the brain. The other cultivates internal thought processes, including daydreaming and imagining, and stretches mainly across the brain\u2019s middle region.</p></div>\n<div class=\"image parbase section\">\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n\n    \n    \n        <span class=\" bumper--bottom bumper--add-margin bumper--bottom_addthis\"></span>\n        <div class=\"cinematic-element fullsize-element \">\n    \n\n    <figure itemscope=\"itemscope\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"image media-image media--cinematic left   \">\n\n        \n            <div id=\"87t6lz9d\" data-pagewide-presentation-disabled=\"false\">\n                \n                    \n                        <div class=\"placeholder-image-wrap\" style=\"padding-bottom: 75.0%\">\n                            <div class=\"picturefill\" data-pestle-module=\"PictureFill\">\n  <script type=\"text/json\" data-pestle-options>\n  {\"alt\":\"Picture of       \n\n        \n            <figcaption class=\"media__caption \">\n                <div class=\"media__caption--text\">\n                    \n                    \n                        <span itemprop=\"caption\">Prodigious productivity is a defining characteristic of genius. Charcoal sketches cover the walls of a once concealed room beneath the Medici Chapel in Florence, where Michelangelo hid for three months in 1530 after defying his patrons. The drawings include a sketch of a seated figure (right) who appears on a tomb in the chapel above.</span>\n                    \n                    \n                    \n                </div>\n            </figcaption>\n        \n\n        \n        \n        \n\n    </figure>\n\n\n\t\n\t\n\t    </div>\n        <span class=\"bumper--top bumper--top_addthis\"></span>\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Jazz improvisation provides a compelling example of how neural networks interact during the creative process. Charles Limb, a hearing specialist and auditory surgeon at UC San Francisco, designed an iron-free keyboard small enough to be played inside the confines of an MRI scanner. Six jazz pianists were asked to play a scale and a piece of memorized music and then to improvise solos as they listened to the sounds of a jazz quartet. Their scans demonstrate that brain activity was \u201cfundamentally different\u201d while the musicians were improvising, says Limb. The internal network, associated with self-expression, showed increased activity, while the outer network, linked to focused attention and also self-censoring, quieted down. \u201cIt\u2019s almost as if the brain turned off its own ability to criticize itself,\u201d he says.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>This may help explain the astounding performances of jazz pianist Keith Jarrett. Jarrett, who improvises concerts that last for as long as two hours, finds it difficult\u2014impossible, actually\u2014to explain how his music takes shape. But when he sits down in front of audiences, he purposefully pushes notes out of his mind, moving his hands to keys he had no intention of playing. \u201cI\u2019m bypassing the brain completely,\u201d he tells me. \u201cI am being pulled by a force that I can only be thankful for.\u201d Jarrett specifically remembers one concert in Munich, where he felt as if he had disappeared into the high notes of the keyboard. His creative artistry, nurtured by decades of listening, learning, and practicing melodies, emerges when he is least in control. \u201cIt\u2019s a vast space in which I trust there will be music,\u201d he says.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>One sign of creativity is being able to make connections between seemingly disparate concepts. Richer communication between areas of the brain may help make those intuitive leaps possible. Andrew Newberg, director of research at the Marcus Institute of Integrative Health at Thomas Jefferson University Hospitals, is using diffusion tensor imaging, an MRI contrast technique, to map neural pathways in the brains of creative people. His participants, who come from Kaufman\u2019s pool of big thinkers, are given standard creativity tests, which ask them to come up with novel uses for everyday objects like baseball bats and toothbrushes. Newberg aims to compare the connectivity in the brains of these high achievers against that of a group of controls to see if there is a difference in how effectively the various regions of their brains interact. His ultimate goal is to scan as many as 25 in each category and then pool the data so he can look for similarities within each group as well as differences that may appear across vocations. For instance, are certain areas more active in a comedian\u2019s brain compared with a psychologist\u2019s?</p></div>\n<div class=\"image parbase section\">\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n\n    \n    \n\n    <figure itemscope=\"itemscope\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"image media-image media--small left   \">\n\n        \n            <div id=\"bftjkeld\" data-pagewide-presentation-disabled=\"false\">\n                \n                    \n                        <div class=\"placeholder-image-wrap\" style=\"padding-bottom: 75.0%\">\n                            <div class=\"picturefill\" data-pestle-module=\"PictureFill\">\n  <script type=\"text/json\" data-pestle-options>\n  {\"alt\":\"Picture of       \n\n        \n            <figcaption class=\"media__caption \">\n                <div class=\"media__caption--text\">\n                    \n                    \n                        <span itemprop=\"caption\">Some 10,000 pairs of identical and fraternal twins are part of geneticist Robert Plomin\u2019s longitudinal study at King\u2019s College London, providing clues about how genes and environment affect development. The genetics of intelligence are enormously complex. \u201cMost geniuses,\u201d says Plomin, \u201cdon\u2019t come from genius parents.\u201d</span>\n                    \n                    \n                    \n                </div>\n            </figcaption>\n        \n\n        \n        \n        \n\n    </figure>\n\n\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>A preliminary comparison of one \u201cgenius\u201d\u2014Newberg uses the word loosely to distinguish the two groups of participants\u2014and one control reveals an intriguing contrast. On the subjects\u2019 brain scans, swaths of red, green, and blue illuminate tracts of white matter, which contain the wiring that allows neurons to transmit electrical messages. The red blotch on each image is the corpus callosum, a centrally located bundle of more than 200 million nerve fibers that joins the two hemispheres of the brain and facilitates connectivity between them. \u201cThe more red you see,\u201d Newberg says, \u201cthe more connecting fibers there are.\u201d The difference is notable: The red section of the \u201cgenius\u201d brain appears to be about twice as wide as the red of the control brain.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>\u201cThis implies that there\u2019s more communication going on between the left and the right hemispheres, which one might expect in people who are highly creative,\u201d says Newberg, stressing that this is an ongoing study. \u201cThere\u2019s more flexibility in their thought processes, more contributions from different parts of the brain.\u201d The green and blue swaths show other areas of connectivity, stretching from front to back\u2014including dialogue among the frontal, parietal, and temporal lobes\u2014and may reveal additional clues, says Newberg. \u201cI don\u2019t know yet what else we might find out. This is just one piece.\u201d</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p><b>Even as neuroscientists </b>try to understand how the brain fosters the development of paradigm-shifting thought processes, other researchers are wrestling with the question of when and from what this capacity develops. Are geniuses born or made? Francis Galton, a cousin of Darwin, objected to what he called \u201cpretensions of natural equality,\u201d believing that genius was passed down through family bloodlines. To prove it, he mapped the lineages of an array of European leaders in disparate fields\u2014from Mozart and Haydn to Byron, Chaucer, Titus, and Napoleon. In 1869 Galton published his results in <i>Hereditary Genius,</i> a book that would launch the \u201cnature versus nurture\u201d debate and spur the misbegotten field of eugenics. Geniuses were rare, Galton concluded, numbering roughly one in a million. What was not unusual, he wrote, were the many instances \u201cin which men who are more or less illustrious have eminent kinsfolk.\u201d</p></div>\n<div class=\"image parbase section\">\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n\n    \n    \n        <span class=\" bumper--bottom bumper--add-margin bumper--bottom_addthis\"></span>\n        <div       \n\n        \n            <figcaption class=\"media__caption \">\n                <div class=\"media__caption--text\">\n                    \n                    \n                        <span itemprop=\"caption\"><p>Stephen Wiltshire, a British artist with autism, created an exquisitely accurate panorama of Mexico City after one afternoon\u2019s viewing and five days of drawing. Psychiatrist Darold Treffert believes that unique wiring between the brain\u2019s right and left hemispheres allows people like Wiltshire to access reserves of creativity. <a href=\"http://news.nationalgeographic.com/2017/04/autism-artist-stephen-wiltshire-cities-genius/\" target=\"_blank\">Watch Wiltshire draw an entire city from memory.</a></p>\n</span>\n                    \n                    \n                    \n                </div>\n            </figcaption>\n        \n\n        \n        \n        \n\n    </figure>\n\n\n\t\n\t\n\t    </div>\n        <span class=\"bumper--top bumper--top_addthis\"></span>\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Advances in genetic research now make it possible to examine human traits at the molecular level. Over the past several decades, scientists have been searching for genes that contribute to intelligence, behavior, and even unique qualities like perfect pitch. In the case of intelligence, this research triggers ethical concerns about how it might be used; it is also exceedingly complex, as thousands of genes may be involved\u2014each one with a very small effect. What about other kinds of abilities? Is there something innate in having an ear for music? Numerous accomplished musicians, including Mozart and Ella Fitzgerald, are believed to have had perfect pitch, which may have played a role in their extraordinary careers.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Genetic potential alone does not predict actual accomplishment. It also takes nurture to grow a genius. Social and cultural influences can provide that nourishment, creating clusters of genius at moments and places in history: Baghdad during Islam\u2019s Golden Age, Kolkata during the Bengal Renaissance, Silicon Valley today.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>A hungry mind can also find the intellectual stimulation it needs at home\u2014as in suburban Adelaide, Australia, in the case of Terence Tao, widely considered one of the greatest minds currently working in mathematics. Tao showed a remarkable grasp of language and numbers early in life, but his parents created the environment in which he could flourish. They provided him with books, toys, and games and encouraged him to play and learn on his own\u2014a practice his father, Billy, believes stimulated his son\u2019s originality and problem-solving skills. Billy and his wife, Grace, also sought out advanced learning opportunities for their son as he began his formal education, and he was fortunate to meet educators who helped foster and stretch his mind. Tao enrolled in high school classes when he was seven years old, scored 760 on the math section of the SAT at age eight, went to university full-time when he was 13, and became a professor at UCLA at 21. \u201cTalent is important,\u201d he once wrote on his blog, \u201cbut how one develops and nurtures it is even more so.\u201d</p></div>\n<div class=\"Interactive section\">\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n src='http://www.nationalgeographic.com/interactive-assets/nggraphics/ngm-genius-age/build-2017-04-21_14-26-50/img/ai2html-graphic-mobile.png'/>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai0-1' class='NGAI-mobile NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:-0.2955%;left:4.6594%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle0'>Age and Achievement </p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai0-2' class='NGAI-mobile NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:1.724%;left:4.487%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>Are you over the hill? Depends on what</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>you&rsquo;re trying to achieve. In 1953</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>psychologist Harvey Lehman published</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>what remains the most comprehensive</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>study of age and achievement. Using the</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>most cited works at the time&mdash;and the age</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>of each work&rsquo;s creator&mdash;he illustrated when</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>outstanding achievement is most likely by</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>discipline. There are exceptions: Giuseppe</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>Verdi composed <em>Aida</em> at 58, two decades</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>older than grand opera&rsquo;s peak age range.</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>Lehman offered 16 factors for why</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>achievement occurs when it does, including</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>declining health and motivation as we age.</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>&emsp;&emsp;Psychologist Dean Keith Simonton, an</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>expert on genius, maintains that the arc of a</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>career depends on two things: your chosen</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>discipline, and how soon you master it.</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>Poets tend to turn ideas into finished works</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>faster than novelists; within a field, geniuses</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>are the quickest studies. &ldquo;Individual</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>differences are actually so large that they</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>swamp the impact of age,&rdquo; wrote Simonton.</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>&ldquo;A first-rate genius at 80 is worth more than</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>a second-rate talent at half that age.&rdquo;</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai0-3' class='NGAI-T-Key_labels NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:28.8649%;left:35.8749%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle2'>Years with highest</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle2'>probability for producing</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle2'>a work of genius</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai0-4' class='NGAI-T-Key_labels of individuals</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai0-5' class='NGAI-T-mobile NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:31.3771%;left:23.118%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>Name</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:-0.6445%;left:-0.0542%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle0'>Age and Achievement </p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai1-2' class='NGAI-T-tablet NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:0.2762%;left:63.7863%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>Marie Curie</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle2'>Discovered radium</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai1-3' class='NGAI-T-labels NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:1.5652%;right:4.621%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle3'>Chemistry</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle4'> works</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle5'>by 46 chemists</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai1-4' class='NGAI-tablet NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:4.051%;left:-0.0417%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>Are you over the hill? Depends on what</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>you&rsquo;re trying to achieve. In 1953</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>psychologist Harvey Lehman published</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>what remains the most comprehensive</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>study of age and achievement. Using the</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>most cited works at the time&mdash;and the age</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>of each work&rsquo;s creator&mdash;he illustrated when</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>outstanding achievement is most likely by</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>discipline. There are exceptions: Giuseppe</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>Verdi composed <em>Aida</em> at 58, two decades</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>older than grand opera&rsquo;s peak age range.</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>Lehman offered 16 factors for why</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>achievement occurs when it does, including</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>declining health and motivation as we age.</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>&emsp;&emsp;Psychologist Dean Keith Simonton, an</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>expert on genius, maintains that the arc of a</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>career depends on two things: your chosen</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>discipline, and how soon you master it.</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>Poets tend to turn ideas into finished works</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>faster than novelists; within a field, geniuses</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>are the quickest studies. &ldquo;Individual</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>differences are actually so large that they</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>swamp the impact of age,&rdquo; wrote Simonton.</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>&ldquo;A first-rate genius at 80 is worth more than</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle6'>a second-rate talent at half that age.&rdquo;</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai1-5' class='NGAI-T-axes NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:6.0765%;left:54.9964%;width:6.0303%;margin-left:-3.0152%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p He</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>found that members of each field created</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>within a web of connections, as shown below</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle1'>for Isaac Newton and Michelangelo.</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai0-3' class='NGAI-T-mobile NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:16.6773%;left:4.8059%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p style='top:59.9777%;left:63.2128%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle2'>1 co-pupil</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle5'>Torrigiano</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai1-19' class='NGAI-T-Big NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:61.6864%;left:36.4784%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle2'>12 admirers</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle5'>Einstein</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle4'>Euler</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle4'>Goethe</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p>&nbsp;</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai1-20' class='NGAI-desktop NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:75.8692%;left:16.3414%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>According to the poet William</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>Words&shy;worth, Isaac Newton was &ldquo;a mind</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>for ever Voyaging through strange seas of</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>Thought, alone.&rdquo; But Newton knew the</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>leading scientists in Europe. He read their</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>work, and they read his. In a letter Newton</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>wrote, &ldquo;If I have seen further it is by</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>standing on the shoulders of Giants.&rdquo;</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t<div id='NGAI-ai1-21' class='NGAI-desktop NGAI-aiAbs' style='top:75.8692%;left:68.2416%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>With help from his father, Michelangelo</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>landed an apprenticeship with Domenico</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>Ghirlandaio, a Florentine painter. The</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>teacher soon sent his gifted student to</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>work in Lorenzo de&rsquo; Medici&rsquo;s sculpture</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>garden. That break immersed</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>Michelangelo in some of the world&rsquo;s</p>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle8'>greatest art&mdash;and its deepest pockets.</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div>\n\t</div>\n\n\n\t<!-- End ai2html - 2017-04-21 - 11:39 -->\n\t<figcaption class=\"media__caption medium-caption\">\n\n    <div class=\"media__caption--text\">           \n\n    \t<small itemprop=\"author\" class=\"media__caption--credit\">Oliver Uberti. Source: DEAN KEITH SIMONTON, PROFESSOR EMERITUS, University of California, DAVIS<br/>Portrait Photos: Paul D. Stewart, Science Source (Newton); Scala/Art Resource, NY (Michelangelo)</small>\n\n     </div>\n\n</figcaption>\n</div>\n\n                    \n                    \n                \n                \n            <figcaption class=\"media__caption medium-caption\">\n                <div class=\"media__caption--text\">\n                    \n                    \n                        \n                    \n                    \n                </div>\n            </figcaption>\n            \n        </div>\n        \n        \n        \n        \n            </div>\n            </div>\n            <span class=\"bumper--top bumper--top_addthis\"></span>\n        \n    \n\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Natural gifts and a nurturing environment can still fall short of producing a genius, without motivation and tenacity propelling one forward. These personality traits, which pushed Darwin to spend two decades perfecting <i>Origin of Species</i> and Indian mathematician Srinivasa Ramanujan to produce thousands of formulas, inspire the work of psychologist Angela Duckworth. She believes that a combination of passion and perseverance\u2014what she calls \u201cgrit\u201d\u2014drives people to achieve. Duckworth, herself a MacArthur Foundation \u201cgenius\u201d and a professor of psychology at the University of Pennsylvania, says the concept of genius is too easily cloaked in layers of magic, as if great achievement erupts spontaneously with no hard work. She believes there are differences when it comes to individual talent, but no matter how brilliant a person, fortitude and discipline are critical to success. \u201cWhen you really look at somebody who accomplishes something great,\u201d she says, \u201cit is not effortless.\u201d</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Nor does it happen on the first try. \u201cThe number one predictor of impact is productivity,\u201d says Dean Keith Simonton, professor emeritus of psychology at UC Davis and a longtime scholar of genius. Big hits emerge after many attempts. \u201cMost articles published in the sciences are never cited by anybody,\u201d says Simonton. \u201cMost compositions are not recorded. Most works of art aren\u2019t displayed.\u201d Thomas Edison invented the phonograph and the first commercially viable light bulb, but these were just two of the thousand-plus U.S. patents he was awarded.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Lack of support can stunt prospects for potential geniuses; they never get the chance to be productive. Throughout history women have been denied formal education, deterred from advancing professionally, and under-recognized for their achievements. Mozart\u2019s older sister, Maria Anna, a brilliant harpsichordist, had her career cut short by her father when she reached the marriageable age of 18. Half the women in the Terman study ended up as homemakers. People born into poverty or oppression don\u2019t get a shot at working toward anything other than staying alive. \u201cIf you do believe that genius is this thing that can be singled out and cultivated and nurtured,\u201d says historian Darrin McMahon, \u201cwhat an incredible tragedy that thousands of geniuses or potential geniuses have withered and died.\u201d</p></div>\n<div style='top:90.2463%;left:4.9554%;'>\n\t\t\t\t<p class='NGAI-aiPstyle4'>Charles Limb (brain image)</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div>\n\t</div>\n\n\n\t<!-- End ai2html - 2017-04-21 - 10:52 -->\n</div>\n\n                    \n                    \n                \n                \n            <figcaption class=\"media__caption medium-caption\">\n                <div class=\"media__caption--text\">\n                    \n                    \n                        <span itemprop=\"caption\"><p>An fMRI brain scan illustrates brain activity during improvisation.</p>\n</span>\n                    \n                        \n                    \n                        <br />\n                    \n                    \n                </div>\n            </figcaption>\n            \n        </div>\n        \n        \n        \n        \n            </div>\n            </div>\n            <span class=\"bumper--top bumper--top_addthis\"></span>\n        \n    \n\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p><b>Sometimes, by sheer </b>good fortune, promise and opportunity collide. If there were ever an individual who personified the concept of genius in every aspect, from its ingredients to its far-reaching impact, it would be Leonardo da Vinci. Born in 1452 to unmarried parents, Leonardo began life in a stone farmhouse in Italy\u2019s Tuscan hills, where olive trees and dusky blue clouds blanket the Arno Valley. From these simple beginnings, Leonardo\u2019s intellect and artistry soared like Schopenhauer\u2019s comet. The breadth of his abilities\u2014his artistic insights, his expertise in human anatomy, his prescient engineering\u2014is unparalleled.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Leonardo\u2019s pathway to genius began with an apprenticeship with master artist Andrea del Verrocchio in Florence when he was a teenager. Leonardo\u2019s creativity was so robust that in his lifetime he filled thousands of pages in his notebooks, which brimmed with studies and designs, from the science of optics to his famed inventions, including a revolving bridge and a flying machine. He persisted no matter the challenge. \u201cObstacles cannot crush me,\u201d he wrote. \u201cHe who is fixed to a star does not change his mind.\u201d Leonardo also lived in a place (Florence) and at a time (the Italian Renaissance) when the arts were cultivated by wealthy patrons and inventiveness coursed through the streets, where great minds, including Michelangelo and Raphael, jostled for acclaim.</p></div>\n<div class=\"inline section\">      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    <span class=\"bumper--bottom bumper--add-margin bumper--bottom_addthis\"></span>\n\n\n\n\n\n    <div class=\"gallery--init\" data-pestle-module=\"InlineGallery\">\n        <script type=\"text/json\" data-pestle-options>\n            {\"id\":\"e8747fab-2ef6-4230-af6a-c82297ee94db/content/inline_546169815\",\"backgroundColor\":\"#f3f1f1\",\"hideForMobile\":false,\"showTitles\":true,\"showAbstracts\":true,\"showCredits\":false,\"showAssetSources\":false,\"showCommerceLinks\":false,\"size\":\"medium\",\"endpoint\":\"http://www.nationalgeographic.com/magazine/2017/05/genius-genetics-intelligence-neuroscience-creativity-einstein/_jcr_content/content/inline_546169815.gallery.json\",\"randomSort\":false,\"data-pagewide-presentation-disabled\":false,\"previewAspectRatio\":\"four-three\",\"title\":{},\"pageTitle\":\"What Makes a Genius?\",\"galleryType\":\"inline\",\"disableEnlarge\":false}\n        </script>\n    </div>\n\n\n\n    <span class=\"bumper--top bumper--top_addthis\"></span>\n\n\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Leonardo delighted in envisioning the impossible\u2014hitting a target that, as Schopenhauer wrote, \u201cothers cannot even see.\u201d Today an international group of scholars and scientists has taken on a similar mission, and its subject is just as elusive: Leonardo himself. The Leonardo Project is tracing the artist\u2019s genealogy and hunting down his DNA to learn more about his ancestry and physical characteristics, to verify paintings that have been attributed to him\u2014and, most remarkably, to search for clues to his extraordinary talent.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>Team member David Caramelli\u2019s high-tech molecular anthropology lab at the University of Florence sits in a 16th-century building with a glorious view of the Florentine skyline. Jutting out majestically is the dome of the city\u2019s prominent cathedral, Santa Maria del Fiore, whose original crowning copper-gilt ball was made by Verrocchio and raised to the top of the cupola with Leonardo\u2019s help in 1471. This juxtaposition of past and present is a fitting setting for Caramelli\u2019s expertise in ancient DNA. Two years ago he published preliminary genetic analyses of a Neanderthal skeleton. Now he is poised to apply similar techniques to Leonardo\u2019s DNA, which the team is hoping to extract from some form of biological relic\u2014the artist\u2019s bones, a strand of hair, skin cells left behind on his paintings or notebooks, or perhaps even saliva, which Leonardo may have used to prepare canvases for his silverpoint drawings.</p></div>\n<div class=\"image parbase section\">\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n\n    \n    \n        <span class=\" bumper--bottom bumper--add-margin bumper--bottom_addthis\"></span>\n        <div class=\"cinematic-element fullsize-element \">\n    \n\n    <figure itemscope=\"itemscope\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"image media-image media--cinematic left   \">\n\n        \n            <div id=\"ly7ebhg9\" data-pagewide-presentation-disabled=\"false\">\n                \n                    \n                        <div class=\"placeholder-image-wrap\" style=\"padding-bottom: 66.63409868099657%\">\n                            <div class=\"picturefill\" data-pestle-module=\"PictureFill\">\n  <script type=\"text/json\" data-pestle-options>\n  {\"alt\":\"Picture of scientist teaching a robot how to recognize and manipulate objects.\",\"imageCrop\":\"\",\"imageboxSize\":\"cinematic\",\"src\":\"http://www.nationalgeographic.com/content/dam/magazine/rights-exempt/2017/05/Genius/genius12-artificial-intelligence-robot.JPG\",\"itemprop\":\"contentUrl\"}\n  </script>\n</div>\n                        </div>\n                    \n                    \n                \n            \n                <script language=\"javascript\">\n  //pass to Prez gallery: Json for photos, current index, the Title for the Prez gallery, social link data, and social link analytics site\n class=\"image-hover-control--hover-only enlarge-button\">\n                    <span>View Images</span>\n                    <span class=\"enlarge-button__icons icon\">\n                      <svg class=\"mt3_icon\">\n                        <use xlink:href=\"#gallery\"></use>\n                      <svg>\n                    </span>\n                </span>\n            \n            </div>\n        \n\n        \n            <figcaption class=\"media__caption \">\n                <div class=\"media__caption--text\">\n                    \n                    \n                        <span itemprop=\"caption\">Throughout history bright minds have flocked to nexuses of creativity like Silicon Valley, where Wenzhao Lian, a researcher at Vicarious, an artificial intelligence company, teaches a robot how to recognize and manipulate objects. The company aims to develop programs that mimic the brain\u2019s capacity for vision, language, and motor control.</span>\n                    \n                    \n                    \n                </div>\n            </figcaption>\n        \n\n        \n        \n        \n\n    </figure>\n\n\n\t\n\t\n\t    </div>\n        <span class=\"bumper--top bumper--top_addthis\"></span>\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>It is an ambitious plan, but team members are optimistically laying the groundwork. Genealogists are tracking down Leonardo\u2019s living relatives on his father\u2019s side for cheek swabs, which Caramelli will use to identify a genetic marker to confirm the authenticity of Leonardo\u2019s DNA if it is found. Physical anthropologists are seeking access to remains that are believed to be Leonardo\u2019s at Amboise castle in France\u2019s Loire Valley, where he was buried in 1519. Art historians and geneticists, including specialists at the institute of genomics pioneer J. Craig Venter, are experimenting with techniques to obtain DNA from fragile Renaissance-era paintings and paper. \u201cThe wheels are starting to turn,\u201d says Jesse Ausubel, vice chairman of the Richard Lounsbery Foundation and an environmental scientist at Rockefeller University in New York City, who is coordinating the project.</p></div>\n<div class=\"image parbase section\">\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n        <span class=\"bumper--bottom bumper--add-margin bumper--bottom_addthis\"></span>\n        <div class=\"fullsize-element \">\n            <div class=\"media--medium__container\">\n    \n\n    \n    \n\n    <figure itemscope=\"itemscope\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"image media-image media--medium left   \">\n\n        \n            <div id=\"fi4iufct\" data-pagewide-presentation-disabled=\"false\">\n                \n                    \n                        <div class=\"placeholder-image-wrap\" style=\"padding-bottom: 71.4704445530044%\">\n                            <div class=\"picturefill\" data-pestle-module=\"PictureFill\">\n  <script type=\"text/json\" data-pestle-options>\n  {\"alt\":\"Picture of mathematician with his formulas written on the blackboard behind him.\",\"imageCrop\":\"\",\"imageboxSize\":\"medium\",\"src\":\"http://www.nationalgeographic.com/content/dam/magazine/rights-exempt/2017/05/Genius/genius13-mathematician-formulas-blackboard.JPG\",\"itemprop\":\"contentUrl\"}\n  </script>\n</div>\n                        </div>\n                    \n                    \n                \n            \n                <script language=\"javascript\">\n                    var el = document.getElementById(\"fi4iufct\");\n                    el.setAttribute('class','standalone-linked');\n                    el.addEventListener('click', function(){\n                        //pass to Prez gallery: Json for photos, current index, the Title for the Prez gallery, social link data, and social link analytics site\n                        Pestle.EventBus.emit(\"OpenPresentationGallery\",\n                                '3d580e4b-21b3-455c-bb25-e7f65df7f56d');\n                    });\n                </script>\n                <span class=\"image-hover-control--hover-only enlarge-button\">\n                    <span>View Images</span>\n                    <span class=\"enlarge-button__icons icon\">\n                      <svg class=\"mt3_icon\">\n                        <use xlink:href=\"#gallery\"></use>\n                      <svg>\n                    </span>\n                </span>\n            \n            </div>\n        \n\n        \n            <figcaption class=\"media__caption medium-caption\">\n                <div class=\"media__caption--text\">\n                    \n                    \n                        <span itemprop=\"caption\">Mathematician Terence Tao\u2019s formulas on fluid dynamics are written on the blackboard behind him. Hailed for his \u201cotherworldly ingenuity,\u201d Tao won the prestigious Fields Medal in 2006 at the age of 31. Yet he rejects lofty notions of genius. What really matters, he writes, is \u201chard work, directed by intuition, literature, and a bit of luck.\u201d</span>\n                    \n                    \n                    \n                </div>\n            </figcaption>\n        \n\n        \n        \n        \n\n    </figure>\n\n\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t         </div>\n\t    </div>\n        <span class=\"bumper--top bumper--top_addthis\"></span>\n\t\n\n</div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>One of the group\u2019s early goals is to explore the possibility that Leonardo\u2019s genius stemmed not only from his intellect, creativity, and cultured environment but also from his exemplary powers of perception. \u201cIn the same way that Mozart may have had extraordinary hearing,\u201d says Ausubel, \u201cLeonardo appears to have had extraordinary visual acuity.\u201d Some of the genetic components of vision are well identified, including the red and green color-vision pigment genes, located on the X chromosome. Thomas Sakmar, a specialist in sensory neuroscience at Rockefeller, says it\u2019s conceivable that scientists could explore those regions of the genome to see if Leonardo had any unique variations that changed his color palette, allowing him to see more hues of red or green than most people are able to perceive.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>The Leonardo Project team doesn\u2019t yet know where to look for answers to other questions, such as how to explain Leonardo\u2019s remarkable ability to visualize birds in flight. \u201cIt\u2019s as if he was creating stroboscopic photographs of stop-action,\u201d says Sakmar. \u201cIt\u2019s not far-fetched that there would be genes related to that ability.\u201d He and his colleagues view their work as the beginning of an expedition that will lead them down new pathways as DNA gives up its secrets.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<p>The quest to unravel the origins of genius may never reach an end point. Like the universe, its mysteries will continue to challenge us, even as we reach for the stars. For some, that is as it should be. \u201cI don\u2019t want to figure it out at all,\u201d says Keith Jarrett when I ask if he is comfortable not knowing how his music takes hold. \u201cIf someone offered me the answer, I\u2019d say, Take it away.\u201d In the end it may be that the journey is illuminating enough and that the insights it reveals along the way\u2014about the brain, about our genes, about the way we think\u2014will nurture glimmers of genius in not just the rare individual but in us all.</p></div>\n<div class=\"parbase smartbody section text\">\n<blockquote>\n To learn more about Albert Einstein, tune in to National Geographic\u2019s 10-part series \n <i><a href=\"http://channel.nationalgeographic.com/genius/\">Genius</a>,</i> which airs Tuesdays starting April 25.\n <br /> \n <b><br /> Claudia Kalb</b> wrote \n <i><a href=\"https://shop.nationalgeographic.com/product/books/books/health-and-wellness/andy-warhol-was-a-hoarder\" target=\"_blank\">Andy Warhol Was a Hoarder: Inside the Minds of History\u2019s Great Personalities</a></i> for National Geographic Books. Photographer \n <b>Paolo Woods</b> lives in Florence, Italy. This is his first story for the magazine.\n <br /> \n <br /> Find out if you could be related to a genius with the new Geno 2.0 kit, available at \n <i><a href=\"https://shop.nationalgeographic.com/product/genographic-2.0-kits/geno-2.0-next-generation-genographic-helix-dna-ancestry-kit--u.s.-delivery?gsk&amp;code=MR21461\" target=\"_blank\">genographic.com/genius</a>.</i>\n</blockquote></div>\n<div class=\"Interactive section\">\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n      \t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n        \n        \n        \n        \n            <span class=\"bumper--bottom bumper--add-margin bumper--bottom_addthis\"></span>\n            <div class=\"cinematic-element fullsize-element \">\n        \n\n        \n        \n\n        <div class=\"media--browser-width clearfix  media--cinematic left \" style=\"padding-top: 0px; padding-bottom: 0px;\">\n            \n            \n                \n                    \n                        <html>\n<a href=\"http://channel.nationalgeographic.com/genius/?source=contentbottombannerpre\"><img src=\"http://www.nationalgeographic.com/content/dam/ngdotcom/rights-exempt/GeniusFolder/genius_1280x320_prePremiere.png\" width=\"100%\" alt=\"Genius. A Global Event Series\"/></a>\n</html>\n                    \n                    \n                \n                \n            <figcaption class=\"media__caption \">\n                <div class=\"media__caption--text\">\n                    \n                    \n                        \n                    \n                    \n                </div>\n            </figcaption>\n type=\"text/javascript\">window.NREUM||(NREUM={});NREUM.info={\"errorBeacon\":\"bam.nr-data.net\",\"licenseKey\":\"114b3a1f69\",\"agent\":\"\",\"beacon\":\"bam.nr-data.net\",\"applicationTime\":484,\"applicationID\":\"28918442\",\"transactionName\":\"NFVSbRBSCxFYAUBeWA0fYlwTRgARTSNAQ0UKUkVNBxwIA14DTl5ZBh9TVg9DCgxcDEBEGBNRV1wWShUHSk1HQ1gRSR9YEEcMAVUH\",\"queueTime\":0}</script>\n</html>\n", "title": "What Makes a Genius?", "url": "http://www.nationalgeographic.com/magazine/2017/05/genius-genetics-intelligence-neuroscience-creativity-einstein.html", "date": "04-21-2017", "source": "national-geographic", "text": "View Images The truest measure of genius is whether a person\u2019s work resonates through the ages. At the Galleria dell\u2019Accademia in Florence, Italy, Michelangelo\u2019s \u201cDavid\u201d towers over admiring visitors more than 500 years after the artist carved the 17-foot-tall statue from a single block of marble discarded by other sculptors.\n\nThis story appears in the May 2017 issue of National Geographic magazine.\n\n\n\nThe M\u00fctter Museum in Philadelphia houses an array of singular medical specimens. On the lower level the fused livers of 19th-century conjoined twins Chang and Eng float in a glass vessel. Nearby, visitors can gawk at hands swollen with gout, the bladder stones of Chief Justice John Marshall, the cancerous tumor extracted from President Grover Cleveland\u2019s jaw, and a thighbone from a Civil War soldier with the wounding bullet still in place. But there\u2019s one exhibit near the entrance that elicits unmatchable awe. Look closely at the display, and you can see smudge marks left by museumgoers pressing their foreheads against the glass.\n\nComing to NG Channel Learn more about \u201cGenius,\u201d premiering April 25, 9/8 C\n\nThe object that fascinates them is a small wooden box containing 46 microscope slides, each displaying a slice of Albert Einstein\u2019s brain. A magnifying glass positioned over one of the slides reveals a piece of tissue about the size of a stamp, its graceful branches and curves resembling an aerial view of an estuary. These remnants of brain tissue are mesmerizing even though\u2014or perhaps because\u2014they reveal little about the physicist\u2019s vaunted powers of cognition. Other displays in the museum show disease and disfigurement\u2014the results of something gone wrong. Einstein\u2019s brain represents potential, the ability of one exceptional mind, one genius, to catapult ahead of everyone else. \u201cHe saw differently from the rest of us,\u201d says visitor Karen O\u2019Hair as she peers at the tea-colored sample. \u201cAnd he could extend beyond that to what he couldn\u2019t see, which is absolutely amazing.\u201d\n\nThroughout history rare individuals have stood out for their meteoric contributions to a field. Lady Murasaki for her literary inventiveness. Michelangelo for his masterful touch. Marie Curie for her scientific acuity. \u201cThe genius,\u201d wrote German philosopher Arthur Schopenhauer, \u201clights on his age like a comet into the paths of the planets.\u201d Consider Einstein\u2019s impact on physics. With no tools at his disposal other than the force of his own thoughts, he predicted in his general theory of relativity that massive accelerating objects\u2014like black holes orbiting each other\u2014would create ripples in the fabric of space-time. It took one hundred years, enormous computational power, and massively sophisticated technology to definitively prove him right, with the physical detection of such gravitational waves less than two years ago.\n\nEinstein revolutionized our understanding of the very laws of the universe. But our understanding of how a mind like his works remains stubbornly earthbound. What set his brainpower, his thought processes, apart from those of his merely brilliant peers? What makes a genius?\n\nPhilosophers have long been pondering the origins of genius. Early Greek thinkers believed an overabundance of black bile\u2014one of the four bodily humors proposed by Hippocrates\u2014endowed poets, philosophers, and other eminent souls with \u201cexalted powers,\u201d says historian Darrin McMahon, author of Divine Fury: A History of Genius. Phrenologists attempted to find genius in bumps on the head; craniometrists collected skulls\u2014including philosopher Immanuel Kant\u2019s\u2014which they probed, measured, and weighed.\n\nView Images A century after Einstein predicted the existence of gravitational waves\u2014ripples in the fabric of space-time\u2014 in his general theory of relativity, scientists like Kazuhiro Yamamoto (on bicycle) plan to use the first underground gravitational wave telescope, KAGRA, in Hida, Japan, to explore what he deduced but could not detect.\n\nNone of them discovered a single source of genius, and such a thing is unlikely to be found. Genius is too elusive, too subjective, too wedded to the verdict of history to be easily identified. And it requires the ultimate expression of too many traits to be simplified into the highest point on one human scale. Instead we can try to understand it by unraveling the complex and tangled qualities\u2014intelligence, creativity, perseverance, and simple good fortune, to name a few\u2014that entwine to create a person capable of changing the world.\n\nIntelligence has often been considered the default yardstick of genius\u2014a measurable quality generating tremendous accomplishment. Lewis Terman, the Stanford University psychologist who helped pioneer the IQ test, believed a test that captured intelligence would also reveal genius. In the 1920s he began tracking more than 1,500 Californian schoolkids with IQs generally above 140\u2014a threshold he labeled as \u201cnear genius or genius\u201d\u2014to see how they fared in life and how they compared with other children. Terman and his collaborators followed the participants, nicknamed \u201cTermites,\u201d for their lifetimes and mapped their successes in a series of reports, Genetic Studies of Genius. The group included members of the National Academy of Sciences, politicians, doctors, professors, and musicians. Forty years after the study began, the researchers documented the thousands of academic reports and books they published, as well as the number of patents granted (350) and short stories written (about 400).\n\nBut monumental intelligence on its own is no guarantee of monumental achievement, as Terman and his collaborators would discover. A number of the study\u2019s participants struggled to thrive, despite their towering IQ scores. Several dozen flunked out of college at first. Others, tested for the study but with IQs that weren\u2019t high enough to make the cut, grew up to become renowned in their fields, most famously Luis Alvarez and William Shockley, both of whom won Nobel Prizes in physics. There\u2019s precedent for such underestimation: Charles Darwin recalled being considered \u201ca very ordinary boy, rather below the common standard in intellect.\u201d As an adult he solved the mystery of how the splendid diversity of life came into being.\n\nView Images Unexpected flashes of insight still require some thought. After seeing an apple fall perpendicularly to the ground in 1666, Isaac Newton reasoned that, in a friend\u2019s telling, \u201cthere must be a drawing power in matter.\u201d The tree that sparked his law of gravity remains rooted next to his childhood home at Woolsthorpe Manor, England.\n\nScientific breakthroughs like Darwin\u2019s theory of evolution by natural selection would be impossible without creativity, a strand of genius that Terman couldn\u2019t measure. But creativity and its processes can be explained, to a certain extent, by creative people themselves. Scott Barry Kaufman, scientific director of the Imagination Institute in Philadelphia, has been bringing together individuals who stand out as trailblazers in their fields\u2014people like psychologist Steven Pinker and comedian Anne Libera of the Second City\u2014to talk about how their ideas and insights are kindled. Kaufman\u2019s goal is not to elucidate genius\u2014he considers the word to be a societal judgment that elevates a chosen few while overlooking others\u2014but to nurture imagination in everyone.\n\nThese discussions have revealed that the aha moment, the flash of clarity that arises at unexpected times\u2014in a dream, in the shower, on a walk\u2014often emerges after a period of contemplation. Information comes in consciously, but the problem is processed unconsciously, the resulting solution leaping out when the mind least expects it. \u201cGreat ideas don\u2019t tend to come when you\u2019re narrowly focusing on them,\u201d says Kaufman.\n\nStudies of the brain offer hints at how these aha moments might happen. The creative process, says Rex Jung, a neuroscientist at the University of New Mexico, relies on the dynamic interplay of neural networks operating in concert and drawing from different parts of the brain at once\u2014both the right and left hemispheres and especially regions in the prefrontal cortex. One of these networks fosters our ability to meet external demands\u2014activities we must act on, like going to work and paying our taxes\u2014and resides largely in outer areas of the brain. The other cultivates internal thought processes, including daydreaming and imagining, and stretches mainly across the brain\u2019s middle region.\n\nView Images Prodigious productivity is a defining characteristic of genius. Charcoal sketches cover the walls of a once concealed room beneath the Medici Chapel in Florence, where Michelangelo hid for three months in 1530 after defying his patrons. The drawings include a sketch of a seated figure (right) who appears on a tomb in the chapel above.\n\nJazz improvisation provides a compelling example of how neural networks interact during the creative process. Charles Limb, a hearing specialist and auditory surgeon at UC San Francisco, designed an iron-free keyboard small enough to be played inside the confines of an MRI scanner. Six jazz pianists were asked to play a scale and a piece of memorized music and then to improvise solos as they listened to the sounds of a jazz quartet. Their scans demonstrate that brain activity was \u201cfundamentally different\u201d while the musicians were improvising, says Limb. The internal network, associated with self-expression, showed increased activity, while the outer network, linked to focused attention and also self-censoring, quieted down. \u201cIt\u2019s almost as if the brain turned off its own ability to criticize itself,\u201d he says.\n\nThis may help explain the astounding performances of jazz pianist Keith Jarrett. Jarrett, who improvises concerts that last for as long as two hours, finds it difficult\u2014impossible, actually\u2014to explain how his music takes shape. But when he sits down in front of audiences, he purposefully pushes notes out of his mind, moving his hands to keys he had no intention of playing. \u201cI\u2019m bypassing the brain completely,\u201d he tells me. \u201cI am being pulled by a force that I can only be thankful for.\u201d Jarrett specifically remembers one concert in Munich, where he felt as if he had disappeared into the high notes of the keyboard. His creative artistry, nurtured by decades of listening, learning, and practicing melodies, emerges when he is least in control. \u201cIt\u2019s a vast space in which I trust there will be music,\u201d he says.\n\nOne sign of creativity is being able to make connections between seemingly disparate concepts. Richer communication between areas of the brain may help make those intuitive leaps possible. Andrew Newberg, director of research at the Marcus Institute of Integrative Health at Thomas Jefferson University Hospitals, is using diffusion tensor imaging, an MRI contrast technique, to map neural pathways in the brains of creative people. His participants, who come from Kaufman\u2019s pool of big thinkers, are given standard creativity tests, which ask them to come up with novel uses for everyday objects like baseball bats and toothbrushes. Newberg aims to compare the connectivity in the brains of these high achievers against that of a group of controls to see if there is a difference in how effectively the various regions of their brains interact. His ultimate goal is to scan as many as 25 in each category and then pool the data so he can look for similarities within each group as well as differences that may appear across vocations. For instance, are certain areas more active in a comedian\u2019s brain compared with a psychologist\u2019s?\n\nView Images Some 10,000 pairs of identical and fraternal twins are part of geneticist Robert Plomin\u2019s longitudinal study at King\u2019s College London, providing clues about how genes and environment affect development. The genetics of intelligence are enormously complex. \u201cMost geniuses,\u201d says Plomin, \u201cdon\u2019t come from genius parents.\u201d\n\nA preliminary comparison of one \u201cgenius\u201d\u2014Newberg uses the word loosely to distinguish the two groups of participants\u2014and one control reveals an intriguing contrast. On the subjects\u2019 brain scans, swaths of red, green, and blue illuminate tracts of white matter, which contain the wiring that allows neurons to transmit electrical messages. The red blotch on each image is the corpus callosum, a centrally located bundle of more than 200 million nerve fibers that joins the two hemispheres of the brain and facilitates connectivity between them. \u201cThe more red you see,\u201d Newberg says, \u201cthe more connecting fibers there are.\u201d The difference is notable: The red section of the \u201cgenius\u201d brain appears to be about twice as wide as the red of the control brain.\n\n\u201cThis implies that there\u2019s more communication going on between the left and the right hemispheres, which one might expect in people who are highly creative,\u201d says Newberg, stressing that this is an ongoing study. \u201cThere\u2019s more flexibility in their thought processes, more contributions from different parts of the brain.\u201d The green and blue swaths show other areas of connectivity, stretching from front to back\u2014including dialogue among the frontal, parietal, and temporal lobes\u2014and may reveal additional clues, says Newberg. \u201cI don\u2019t know yet what else we might find out. This is just one piece.\u201d\n\nEven as neuroscientists try to understand how the brain fosters the development of paradigm-shifting thought processes, other researchers are wrestling with the question of when and from what this capacity develops. Are geniuses born or made? Francis Galton, a cousin of Darwin, objected to what he called \u201cpretensions of natural equality,\u201d believing that genius was passed down through family bloodlines. To prove it, he mapped the lineages of an array of European leaders in disparate fields\u2014from Mozart and Haydn to Byron, Chaucer, Titus, and Napoleon. In 1869 Galton published his results in Hereditary Genius, a book that would launch the \u201cnature versus nurture\u201d debate and spur the misbegotten field of eugenics. Geniuses were rare, Galton concluded, numbering roughly one in a million. What was not unusual, he wrote, were the many instances \u201cin which men who are more or less illustrious have eminent kinsfolk.\u201d\n\nView Images Stephen Wiltshire, a British artist with autism, created an exquisitely accurate panorama of Mexico City after one afternoon\u2019s viewing and five days of drawing. Psychiatrist Darold Treffert believes that unique wiring between the brain\u2019s right and left hemispheres allows people like Wiltshire to access reserves of creativity. Watch Wiltshire draw an entire city from memory.\n\nAdvances in genetic research now make it possible to examine human traits at the molecular level. Over the past several decades, scientists have been searching for genes that contribute to intelligence, behavior, and even unique qualities like perfect pitch. In the case of intelligence, this research triggers ethical concerns about how it might be used; it is also exceedingly complex, as thousands of genes may be involved\u2014each one with a very small effect. What about other kinds of abilities? Is there something innate in having an ear for music? Numerous accomplished musicians, including Mozart and Ella Fitzgerald, are believed to have had perfect pitch, which may have played a role in their extraordinary careers.\n\nGenetic potential alone does not predict actual accomplishment. It also takes nurture to grow a genius. Social and cultural influences can provide that nourishment, creating clusters of genius at moments and places in history: Baghdad during Islam\u2019s Golden Age, Kolkata during the Bengal Renaissance, Silicon Valley today.\n\nA hungry mind can also find the intellectual stimulation it needs at home\u2014as in suburban Adelaide, Australia, in the case of Terence Tao, widely considered one of the greatest minds currently working in mathematics. Tao showed a remarkable grasp of language and numbers early in life, but his parents created the environment in which he could flourish. They provided him with books, toys, and games and encouraged him to play and learn on his own\u2014a practice his father, Billy, believes stimulated his son\u2019s originality and problem-solving skills. Billy and his wife, Grace, also sought out advanced learning opportunities for their son as he began his formal education, and he was fortunate to meet educators who helped foster and stretch his mind. Tao enrolled in high school classes when he was seven years old, scored 760 on the math section of the SAT at age eight, went to university full-time when he was 13, and became a professor at UCLA at 21. \u201cTalent is important,\u201d he once wrote on his blog, \u201cbut how one develops and nurtures it is even more so.\u201d\n\nAge and Achievement Are you over the hill? Depends on what you\u2019re trying to achieve. In 1953 psychologist Harvey Lehman published what remains the most comprehensive study of age and achievement. Using the most cited works at the time\u2014and the age of each work\u2019s creator\u2014he illustrated when outstanding achievement is most likely by discipline. There are exceptions: Giuseppe Verdi composed Aida at 58, two decades older than grand opera\u2019s peak age range. Lehman offered 16 factors for why achievement occurs when it does, including declining health and motivation as we age. Psychologist Dean Keith Simonton, an expert on genius, maintains that the arc of a career depends on two things: your chosen discipline, and how soon you master it. Poets tend to turn ideas into finished works faster than novelists; within a field, geniuses are the quickest studies. \u201cIndividual differences are actually so large that they swamp the impact of age,\u201d wrote Simonton. \u201cA first-rate genius at 80 is worth more than a second-rate talent at half that age.\u201d Years with highest probability for producing a work of genius Discipline Number of works by number of individuals Name Work 100% AGE 20 30 40 50 60 A LIFE\u2019S WORK Lehman divided the number of works produced in each discipline during each five-year interval by the number of creators who were alive in that span. He charted those rates as percentages of peak output.
