Lehman\n\nSocial Networks of Genius Lone geniuses are exceedingly rare. Dean Keith Simonton scoured biographical dictionaries for mentions of relationships among 2,026 scientists and 772 artists. He found that members of each field created within a web of connections, as shown below for Isaac Newton and Michelangelo. 14 idols, including Bacon Copernicus Descartes Galileo Kepler 3 rivals Hooke Huygens Leibniz 1 mentor Barrow 1 collaborator Halley 10 associates Flamsteed Newton 1 friend Locke 12 admirers Einstein Euler Goethe According to the poet William Words\u00adworth, Isaac Newton was \u201ca mind for ever Voyaging through strange seas of Thought, alone.\u201d But Newton knew the leading scientists in Europe. He read their work, and they read his. In a letter Newton wrote, \u201cIf I have seen further it is by standing on the shoulders of Giants.\u201d 8 idols Donatello Ghiberti Giotto Masaccio 2 rivals Leonardo Signorelli 2 mentors Bertoldo Ghirlandaio 1 collaborator Sebastiano Michelangelo 2 apprentices Cellini Condivi 1 friend Vasari 1 co-pupil Torrigiano 40 admirers Rubens Titian Vel\u00e1zquez With help from his father, Michelangelo landed an apprenticeship with Domenico Ghirlandaio, a Florentine painter. The teacher soon sent his gifted student to work in Lorenzo de\u2019 Medici\u2019s sculpture garden. Source: DEAN KEITH SIMONTON, PROFESSOR EMERITUS, University of California, DAVIS\n\nPortrait Photos: Paul D. Stewart, Science Source (Newton); Scala/Art Resource, NY (Michelangelo)\n\nNatural gifts and a nurturing environment can still fall short of producing a genius, without motivation and tenacity propelling one forward. These personality traits, which pushed Darwin to spend two decades perfecting Origin of Species and Indian mathematician Srinivasa Ramanujan to produce thousands of formulas, inspire the work of psychologist Angela Duckworth. She believes that a combination of passion and perseverance\u2014what she calls \u201cgrit\u201d\u2014drives people to achieve. Duckworth, herself a MacArthur Foundation \u201cgenius\u201d and a professor of psychology at the University of Pennsylvania, says the concept of genius is too easily cloaked in layers of magic, as if great achievement erupts spontaneously with no hard work. She believes there are differences when it comes to individual talent, but no matter how brilliant a person, fortitude and discipline are critical to success. \u201cWhen you really look at somebody who accomplishes something great,\u201d she says, \u201cit is not effortless.\u201d\n\nNor does it happen on the first try. \u201cThe number one predictor of impact is productivity,\u201d says Dean Keith Simonton, professor emeritus of psychology at UC Davis and a longtime scholar of genius. Big hits emerge after many attempts. \u201cMost articles published in the sciences are never cited by anybody,\u201d says Simonton. \u201cMost compositions are not recorded. Most works of art aren\u2019t displayed.\u201d Thomas Edison invented the phonograph and the first commercially viable light bulb, but these were just two of the thousand-plus U.S. patents he was awarded.\n\nLack of support can stunt prospects for potential geniuses; they never get the chance to be productive. Throughout history women have been denied formal education, deterred from advancing professionally, and under-recognized for their achievements. Mozart\u2019s older sister, Maria Anna, a brilliant harpsichordist, had her career cut short by her father when she reached the marriageable age of 18. Half the women in the Terman study ended up as homemakers. People born into poverty or oppression don\u2019t get a shot at working toward anything other than staying alive. \u201cIf you do believe that genius is this thing that can be singled out and cultivated and nurtured,\u201d says historian Darrin McMahon, \u201cwhat an incredible tragedy that thousands of geniuses or potential geniuses have withered and died.\u201d\n\nLateral prefrontal deactivation Self-monitoring Medial prefrontal activation Self-expression Charles Limb (brain image) Lateral prefrontal deactivation Self-monitoring Medial prefrontal activation Self-expression Charles Limb (brain image) An fMRI brain scan illustrates brain activity during improvisation.\n\n\n\nSometimes, by sheer good fortune, promise and opportunity collide. If there were ever an individual who personified the concept of genius in every aspect, from its ingredients to its far-reaching impact, it would be Leonardo da Vinci. Born in 1452 to unmarried parents, Leonardo began life in a stone farmhouse in Italy\u2019s Tuscan hills, where olive trees and dusky blue clouds blanket the Arno Valley. From these simple beginnings, Leonardo\u2019s intellect and artistry soared like Schopenhauer\u2019s comet. The breadth of his abilities\u2014his artistic insights, his expertise in human anatomy, his prescient engineering\u2014is unparalleled.\n\nLeonardo\u2019s pathway to genius began with an apprenticeship with master artist Andrea del Verrocchio in Florence when he was a teenager. Leonardo\u2019s creativity was so robust that in his lifetime he filled thousands of pages in his notebooks, which brimmed with studies and designs, from the science of optics to his famed inventions, including a revolving bridge and a flying machine. He persisted no matter the challenge. \u201cObstacles cannot crush me,\u201d he wrote. \u201cHe who is fixed to a star does not change his mind.\u201d Leonardo also lived in a place (Florence) and at a time (the Italian Renaissance) when the arts were cultivated by wealthy patrons and inventiveness coursed through the streets, where great minds, including Michelangelo and Raphael, jostled for acclaim.\n\nLeonardo delighted in envisioning the impossible\u2014hitting a target that, as Schopenhauer wrote, \u201cothers cannot even see.\u201d Today an international group of scholars and scientists has taken on a similar mission, and its subject is just as elusive: Leonardo himself. The Leonardo Project is tracing the artist\u2019s genealogy and hunting down his DNA to learn more about his ancestry and physical characteristics, to verify paintings that have been attributed to him\u2014and, most remarkably, to search for clues to his extraordinary talent.\n\nTeam member David Caramelli\u2019s high-tech molecular anthropology lab at the University of Florence sits in a 16th-century building with a glorious view of the Florentine skyline. Jutting out majestically is the dome of the city\u2019s prominent cathedral, Santa Maria del Fiore, whose original crowning copper-gilt ball was made by Verrocchio and raised to the top of the cupola with Leonardo\u2019s help in 1471. This juxtaposition of past and present is a fitting setting for Caramelli\u2019s expertise in ancient DNA. Two years ago he published preliminary genetic analyses of a Neanderthal skeleton. Now he is poised to apply similar techniques to Leonardo\u2019s DNA, which the team is hoping to extract from some form of biological relic\u2014the artist\u2019s bones, a strand of hair, skin cells left behind on his paintings or notebooks, or perhaps even saliva, which Leonardo may have used to prepare canvases for his silverpoint drawings.\n\nView Images Throughout history bright minds have flocked to nexuses of creativity like Silicon Valley, where Wenzhao Lian, a researcher at Vicarious, an artificial intelligence company, teaches a robot how to recognize and manipulate objects. The company aims to develop programs that mimic the brain\u2019s capacity for vision, language, and motor control.\n\nIt is an ambitious plan, but team members are optimistically laying the groundwork. Genealogists are tracking down Leonardo\u2019s living relatives on his father\u2019s side for cheek swabs, which Caramelli will use to identify a genetic marker to confirm the authenticity of Leonardo\u2019s DNA if it is found. Physical anthropologists are seeking access to remains that are believed to be Leonardo\u2019s at Amboise castle in France\u2019s Loire Valley, where he was buried in 1519. Art historians and geneticists, including specialists at the institute of genomics pioneer J. Craig Venter, are experimenting with techniques to obtain DNA from fragile Renaissance-era paintings and paper. \u201cThe wheels are starting to turn,\u201d says Jesse Ausubel, vice chairman of the Richard Lounsbery Foundation and an environmental scientist at Rockefeller University in New York City, who is coordinating the project.\n\nView Images Mathematician Terence Tao\u2019s formulas on fluid dynamics are written on the blackboard behind him. Hailed for his \u201cotherworldly ingenuity,\u201d Tao won the prestigious Fields Medal in 2006 at the age of 31. Yet he rejects lofty notions of genius. What really matters, he writes, is \u201chard work, directed by intuition, literature, and a bit of luck.\u201d\n\nOne of the group\u2019s early goals is to explore the possibility that Leonardo\u2019s genius stemmed not only from his intellect, creativity, and cultured environment but also from his exemplary powers of perception. \u201cIn the same way that Mozart may have had extraordinary hearing,\u201d says Ausubel, \u201cLeonardo appears to have had extraordinary visual acuity.\u201d Some of the genetic components of vision are well identified, including the red and green color-vision pigment genes, located on the X chromosome. Thomas Sakmar, a specialist in sensory neuroscience at Rockefeller, says it\u2019s conceivable that scientists could explore those regions of the genome to see if Leonardo had any unique variations that changed his color palette, allowing him to see more hues of red or green than most people are able to perceive.\n\nThe Leonardo Project team doesn\u2019t yet know where to look for answers to other questions, such as how to explain Leonardo\u2019s remarkable ability to visualize birds in flight. \u201cIt\u2019s as if he was creating stroboscopic photographs of stop-action,\u201d says Sakmar. \u201cIt\u2019s not far-fetched that there would be genes related to that ability.\u201d He and his colleagues view their work as the beginning of an expedition that will lead them down new pathways as DNA gives up its secrets.\n\nThe quest to unravel the origins of genius may never reach an end point. Like the universe, its mysteries will continue to challenge us, even as we reach for the stars. For some, that is as it should be. \u201cI don\u2019t want to figure it out at all,\u201d says Keith Jarrett when I ask if he is comfortable not knowing how his music takes hold. \u201cIf someone offered me the answer, I\u2019d say, Take it away.\u201d In the end it may be that the journey is illuminating enough and that the insights it reveals along the way\u2014about the brain, about our genes, about the way we think\u2014will nurture glimmers of genius in not just the rare individual but in us all.", "images": ["http://www.nationalgeographic.com/interactive-assets/nggraphics/ngm-genius-network/build-2017-04-21_11-40-25/img/ai2html-graphic-network-mobile.png", "http://www.nationalgeographic.com/interactive-assets/nggraphics/ngm-genius-network/build-2017-04-21_11-40-25/img/ai2html-graphic-network-tablet-desktop.png", "http://www.nationalgeographic.com/interactive-assets/nggraphics/ngm-genius-brain/build-2017-04-21_10-58-34/img/ai2html-graphic-brain-tablet-desktop.png", "http://www.nationalgeographic.com/interactive-assets/nggraphics/ngm-genius-brain/build-2017-04-21_10-58-34/img/ai2html-graphic-brain-mobile.png", "http://www.nationalgeographic.com/etc/designs/platform/v2/images/logos/ng-black-logo.ngsversion.45ded159.png", "http://www.nationalgeographic.com/interactive-assets/nggraphics/ngm-genius-age/build-2017-04-21_14-26-50/img/ai2html-graphic-mobile.png", "http://www.nationalgeographic.com/interactive-assets/nggraphics/ngm-genius-age/build-2017-04-21_14-26-50/img/ai2html-graphic-tablet-desktop.png", "http://www.nationalgeographic.com/content/dam/magazine/rights-exempt/2017/05/Genius/genius1-michelangelo-david-florence-italy.ngsversion.1492799627302.adapt.1900.1.jpg", "http://www.nationalgeographic.com/content/dam/ngdotcom/rights-exempt/GeniusFolder/genius_1280x320_prePremiere.png"], "videos": []}
